Critique of Biased Scholarship: The Paradise Marriage Story
Buti exposes how a Bedouin's question was misrepresented as a scholarly debate to advance a political agenda.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Buti exposes a case of flawed scholarship where a weak anecdote was generalized to support a political agenda.

Core Idea
One Bedouin's question was twisted into a false scholarly consensus.

This passage from Buti's work is a sharp methodological critique. He shows how two orientalists, Von Kremer and Goldziher, cited a story about whether non-Arabs would marry Arab women in Paradise as evidence that Islamic conquests were driven by Arab nationalism. Buti traces the story to its source and reveals it was a weak report of a single Bedouin's query, not a scholarly debate. This lesson examines Buti's deconstruction and the lesson it teaches about scholarly integrity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the emotional tone at the end.
The passage uses rhetorical questioning and exclamation.
2
Identify the original claim
What did Von Kremer and Goldziher claim was discussed? What was their motive according to Buti?
3
Trace Buti's investigation
Buti first notes the reader's likely impression. Then he investigates the source. What does he find?
Pay attention to the chain: from 'people' to one Bedouin.
4
Understand the critique of methodology
Buti criticizes how the report was presented. List the three flaws: cut from source, generalized, forced to yield a desired conclusion.
The phrase 'مستكرهاً على أن ينطق رغماً عن أنفه' is key.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this case illustrates the importance of source verification and avoiding bias in research.
This connects to broader principles of Islamic scholarship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١ - ينقل فون كريمر وغولد زيهر أن الناس بحثوا في موضوع غريب وهو : هل ينكح العجم نساء العرب في الجنة ؟ وذلك رغبة منها في إثبات أن الفتوحات الإسلامية إنما يكمن وراءها القصد إلى السيادة العربية)
ولا ريب أن الذي يقرأ هذا النص إنما يتصور أن جمهرة من الناس بحثوا هذا الموضوع ، وأن الذين بحثوه إنما هم الفقهاء ، إذ هو مما يخص الفقهاء قبل غيرهم . ولكنك إذا رجعت إلى مصدر القصة وسندها وحقيقتها ، علمت أن ( الناس ( الذين بحثوا موضوع زواج غير العرب من العربيات في الجنة إنما هم ) أعرابي واحد ) جاء من البادية، سمع الأصمعي يقول لآخر : أترى هذه العجم تنكح نساءنا في الجنة ؟ فقال أرى ذلك والله بالعمل الصالح . وهي قصة رواها المبرد في الكامل مضعفاً ثبوتها (")
فتأمل في كيفية سوق الخبر مقطوعاً عن مصدره ، معروضاً بصيغة التعميم ، مستكرهاً على أن ينطق رغماً عن أنفه بالشهادة التي يريدها الباحث العلمي الموضوعي النزيه !!
1 - Von Kremer and Goldziher report that people discussed a strange topic: Do non-Arabs marry Arab women in Paradise? This was out of a desire to prove that the Islamic conquests were driven by the intention of Arab supremacy. There is no doubt that whoever reads this text imagines that a multitude of people discussed this topic, and that those who discussed it were the jurists, for it is a matter that concerns jurists before others. But if you return to the source of the story, its chain of transmission, and its reality, you will know that 'the people' who discussed the topic of non-Arabs marrying Arab women in Paradise were just one Bedouin who came from the desert; al-Asma'i heard him saying to another: Do you think these non-Arabs will marry our women in Paradise? He said: I think so, by Allah, through righteous deeds. And it is a story reported by al-Mubarrad in al-Kamil, considering its authenticity weak. So reflect on how the report was presented cut off from its source, presented in a generalized form, forced to speak reluctantly the testimony that the objective, impartial scientific researcher desires!!
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is an example of flawed methodology in academic research. The author cites a claim by orientialists Von Kremer and Goldziher that 'people' discussed whether non-Arabs would marry Arab women in Paradise, which they used to argue that Islamic conquests were motivated by Arab nationalism. The author then deconstructs this claim by tracing it to its source in al-Mubarrad's al-Kamil. He reveals that the 'people' were actually a single Bedouin, and the story is considered weak. The author criticizes how the report was presented: taken out of context, generalized, and forced to yield a desired testimony, thereby violating the principles of objective research.

Key Terms
السيادة العربية
التعميم
الخبر مقطوع عن مصدره
المبرد
الكامل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is criticizing the orientalists for being non-Muslims.
Correction: Buti's critique is about scholarly methodology and objectivity, not faith. He accuses them of biased scholarship, specifically: cutting a report from its source, generalizing a single anecdote, and forcing it to support a political agenda.

Checkpoint
According to Buti, who actually discussed the topic of non-Arabs marrying Arab women in Paradise?
A) A group of fuqaha'
B) A single Bedouin
C) Orientalists
D) The author of al-Kamil

Key Takeaway
A single weak report from a Bedouin was generalized and misrepresented as a scholarly consensus to support a political narrative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a claim about Islam from a biased source, do you ever stop to trace it back to its original context, or do you accept it at face value? What would it take for you to commit to verifying every claim before spreading it?
?
Think of a time you generalized a single experience or rumor into a broad judgment about others. How can the example of the orientalists' misrepresentation of the Bedouin's question serve as a warning to you in your own judgments?
?
This lesson shows how a weak report can be weaponized. Are there areas in your own life where you have built a narrative on weak evidence? What is one step you will take today to correct that?

Critique of the Unsubstantiated Claim about Uthman's Arrangement
Buti critiques an unsupported claim about Uthman's arrangement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Buti critiques a historical claim by exposing its lack of evidentiary methodology, reinforcing the principle that claims without evidence are unacceptable.

Core Idea
Claims without evidence are mere assertions, not scholarship.

In this passage, Buti examines a specific claim about Uthman's arrangement of the Quran. He does not engage with the content of the claim itself but focuses on the method—or lack thereof—behind it. By highlighting that the authors presented a 'bare claim' without any evidence, he teaches us a fundamental lesson in intellectual integrity: every assertion must be supported by proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the claim
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the specific claim made by Gardet and Qanawati about Uthman arranging surahs by length.
2
Understand the author's critique
Buti's main point is that the claim lacks any methodological foundation. He states that the method is 'completely lacking'. Recognize that he is not refuting the claim per se, but the way it is presented.
3
Reflect on the poetic evidence
The poet's line reinforces the idea that claims require clear proofs. Consider how this applies to academic research.
4
Apply the lesson
Think about other examples where claims are made without evidence. How does this principle guard against accepting falsehoods?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢ - جاء في كتاب فلسفة الفكر الديني بين الإسلام والمسيحية للويس غردية وج . قنواتي أن عثمان بن عفان أقبل إلى القرآن في خلافته ، فقسمه إلى سور وآيات ، ورتب السور وراء بعضها حسب طولها ، فأطولها ثم ما دونها طولاً وهكذا [؟]. فتأمل أولاً ، في المنهج المتبع لإثبات هذه الدعوى أو الفرضية فستعلم أن المنهج مفقود من أساسه ، وإنما يضع المؤلفان أمامنا دعوى عارية لنغمض العين ونقبلها كما هي متناسين قول الشاعر: والدعاوى إن لم تقيموا عليها بينات أبناؤها أدعياء
2 - It appears in the book 'Philosophy of Religious Thought between Islam and Christianity' by Louis Gardet and G. Qanawati that Uthman ibn Affan turned to the Quran during his caliphate, divided it into surahs and verses, and arranged the surahs following each other according to their length: the longest, then what is shorter, and so on [؟]. Then consider first the method used to prove this claim or hypothesis; you will know that the method is completely lacking, and the two authors place before us a bare claim for us to close our eyes and accept it as it is, forgetting the poet's saying: 'Claims, if you do not bring clear proofs for them, their proponents are mere claimants.'
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is another example of flawed methodology. The author quotes a claim from Gardet and Qanawati that Uthman arranged the Quran's surahs by length. The author then examines the method used to prove this claim and finds that no method exists at all—the claim is presented without evidence. The authors expect the reader to accept it blindly. The author reinforces his critique with a poetic line underscoring the necessity of evidence for any claim.

Key Terms

بينات
منهج مفقود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Buti is denying that Uthman arranged the Quran in any way.
Correction: Buti is not denying the arrangement; he is criticizing the absence of evidence for the specific claim that Uthman arranged surahs by length. His critique is methodological, not substantive.

Checkpoint

A) The claim is historically inaccurate
B) The claim lacks any evidentiary method or proof
C) The claim contradicts the Quran's own order
D) The claim is found in an unreliable source

Key Takeaway
A claim without evidence is not scholarship; rigorous methodology and clear proofs are essential for any valid assertion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Spend a moment identifying one belief you hold without having personally examined its evidence. What action will you take today to find that evidence?

Refutation of Gibb's Distortion of Shah Waliullah
Buti accuses Gibb of distorting Shah Waliullah's text and presents the correct interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Buti exposes Gibb's deliberate distortion of Shah Waliullah's text and the actual meaning of the passage.

Core Idea
Context is everything: Gibb ripped a quote from Shah Waliullah to support his claim, but the full text refutes him.

This lesson examines a case study in Orientalist methodology. Buti demonstrates how Gibb selectively quoted Shah Waliullah's Hujjat Allah al-Baligha to argue that Islam merely adapted pre-Islamic Arab animism. Buti shows that when the full context is restored, Shah Waliullah actually argues the opposite: that the Prophet came to reform and correct the existing religious practices. This exposure of 'distortion of words from their contexts' is a lesson in scholarly integrity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice Gibb's claim and the specific quote he uses.
Pay attention to Buti's strong language: 'most dangerous betrayal'.
2
Identify Gibb's claim and the quote he used
Gibb claims Islam gave religious character to pre-Islamic animism, and he cites Shah Waliullah to support this. Find the quote that Gibb extracts.
The quote appears in brackets in the translation.
3
Understand Buti's accusation of distortion
Buti accuses Gibb of deliberately taking the quote out of context. What is the basis of his accusation? Look for phrases like 'deliberate distortion of words from their contexts'.
Buti argues that the phrase is immersed in a long discourse and cannot stand alone.
4
Discover Shah Waliullah's actual message
Buti then presents what Shah Waliullah actually says: the Prophet was sent with the pure Hanifiyyah of Isma'il to correct its distortions. How does this refute Gibb's interpretation?
The original text emphasizes reform, not accommodation of existing practices.
5
Reflect on the importance of context
Consider how this case illustrates the danger of quoting out of context. Reflect on the ethical responsibilities of scholars when citing sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقول المستشرق جب في كتابه ( بنية الفكر الديني في الإسلام ) أن الإسلام جاء ليضفي الصفة الدينية على تلك ( الإحيائية ( العربية القديمة التي نسجتها الأعراف والبيئة بعد أن لم يستطع محمد عليه الصلاة والسلام التخلص منها ويمضي يقرر ذلك في منهج - جد غريب وعجيب - من حيث إيغاله في الاستنتاج بل الحدس المجرد في أغلب الأحيان . ولكن ذلك كله في منتهى البساطة بالنسبة لما يلي : يقول جب في مقدمة كتابه هذا : إن الأفكار التي أسست عليها هذه الفصول ليست من بنات دماغي ، بل سبقني إليها ودلني عليها جماعة ، من المفكرين ومن أقطاب المسلمين وقد يطول إحصاؤهم ، فسأكتفي بذكر أحدهم على سبيل المثال هو الشيخ الكبير شاه ولي الله الدهلوي . ثم ينقل عن كتابه ( حجة الله البالغـة ) هذا النص الحرفي مثبتاً بين قوسين كما أنقله للقارئ الكريم : إن النبي له بعث بعثة تتضمن بعثة أخرى . فالأولى إنما كانت إلى بني إسماعيل .. وهذه البعثة تستوجب أن يكون مادة شريعته ما عندهم من الشعائر وسنن العبادات ووجوه الارتفاقات إذ الشرع إنما هو إصلاح ما عندهم ، لا تكليفهم بما لا يعرفونه أصلاً » ونحن نقول : لا ريب أن جب لم يقع على هذه العبارة وحدها من الكتاب دون أن يبصر شيئاً مما قبلها ولا مما بعدها ، فذلك أمر مستحيل إذ العبارة مغمورة في كلام طويل من حولها . وهنا نقف - ويا للأسف - على أخطر خيانة في البحث والنقل ، ألا وهي تعمد تحريف الكلم عن مواضعه وتحميل صاحبها من الوزر ما لم يحمل ، ومحاولة إنطاقه بما هو منه بريء . والعجيب أننا لو رحنا نفتش في كتب السابقين عن رد واف على أخيلة جب التي ساقها في كتابه هذا ، لما وجدنا رداً أبلغ ولا أو فى مما جاء في كتاب الشاه ولي الله الدهلوي في كتابه حجة الله البالغة في نفس الصحيفة التي انتزع منها جب النص ليستشهد فيه على ما يقول . ولكأن الله عز وجل ألهمه أن يقطع بذلك السبيل على من سيأتي ليحمّل كلامه ما لا يحمل وأن ينطقه بما لا يمكن أن ينطق به . وإليك ما يقوله في ذلك : ) اعلم أنه الله بعث بالحنيفية الإسماعيلية لإقامة عوجها وإزالـة تحريفها
The orientalist Gibb says in his book 'The Structure of Religious Thought in Islam' that Islam came to bestow a religious character upon that ancient Arab 'animism' which was woven by customs and environment, after Muhammad, peace be upon him, could not get rid of it. And he proceeds to assert that using a method - very strange and amazing - in terms of his excess in deduction, rather pure intuition most of the time. But all of that is extremely simple compared to what follows: Gibb says in the introduction of his book: 'The ideas upon which these chapters are founded are not the offspring of my brain; rather, a group of thinkers and leading Muslims preceded me to them and guided me to them, and their enumeration would be long, so I will suffice by mentioning one of them as an example, the great Shaykh Shah Waliullah al-Dahlawi.' Then he quotes from his book 'Hujjat Allah al-Baligha' this literal text, placed in brackets, as I quote it for the dear reader: 'The Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, was sent with a mission that includes another mission. The first was to the children of Isma'il... and this mission requires that the material of his law be what they have of rituals, rites of worship, and ways of benefit, for the law is only reforming what they have, not burdening them with what they do not know at all.' And we say: There is no doubt that Gibb did not come upon this phrase alone from the book without seeing anything before it or after it, for that is impossible since the phrase is immersed in a long discourse around it. And here we stand - alas - upon the most dangerous betrayal in research and transmission, namely the deliberate distortion of words from their contexts and loading the author with a burden he did not carry, and attempting to make him say what he is innocent of. And the amazing thing is that if we were to search the books of the predecessors for a sufficient refutation of Gibb's fantasies that he advanced in this book, we would not find a more eloquent nor more complete refutation than what is found in the book of Shah Waliullah al-Dahlawi in his book 'Hujjat Allah al-Baligha' on the very page from which Gibb extracted the text to cite as evidence for his claim. And it is as if Allah, may He be exalted, inspired him to cut off that path for whoever would come later to load his words with what they cannot carry and to make him say what cannot be said. And here is what he says in that regard: 'Know that Allah sent him with the Hanifiyyah of Isma'il to rectify its crookedness and remove its distortion.'
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a critique of Orientalist methodology, specifically targeting W. Montgomery Watt (Gibb) and his misuse of Shah Waliullah al-Dahlawi's text. Buti begins by summarizing Gibb's claim that Islam merely added a religious veneer to pre-Islamic Arabian animism. He then focuses on Gibb's introduction where Gibb cites Shah Waliullah as a source. Gibb selectively quotes a passage from 'Hujjat Allah al-Baligha' that seems to suggest the Prophet's mission accommodated existing Arab practices. Buti accuses Gibb of deliberately distorting the text by taking it out of context. He argues that the phrase is embedded in a long discussion and cannot be understood in isolation. Buti then points out that the best refutation of Gibb's interpretation actually comes from the very same book and page that Gibb used. He claims that Shah Waliullah's full statement - that the Prophet was sent with the pure Hanifiyyah of Isma'il to correct its deviations - directly contradicts Gibb's portrayal. Thus, Buti exposes what he calls a treacherous manipulation of sources.

Key Terms
الاستشراق
تحريف الكلم عن مواضعه
شاه ولي الله الدهلوي
حجة الله البالغة
الحنيفية الإسماعيلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Buti's Critique of Gibb's Neglect
Buti rebukes Gibb for ignoring Dehlawi's clarifying text.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Buti's argument that Gibb deliberately ignored Dehlawi's clarifying text in order to misrepresent him.

Core Idea
Gibb saw the refutation but chose to ignore it.

Buti begins by pointing out a glaring omission in the orientalist Gibb's work. Gibb quoted Dehlawi selectively and then claimed Dehlawi supported his views, but the very text that follows the quoted sentences explicitly contradicts Gibb's interpretation. Buti highlights this as evidence of deliberate distortion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the rhetorical questions.
The passage uses a question to emphasize the absurdity of Gibb's actions.
2
Identify the contradiction
Identify what Gibb ignored and how it contradicts his claim. The text after the quoted sentences is a detailed explanation of them.
Buti expects every contemplative reader to see this.
3
Reflect on the accusation
Reflect on why Buti accuses Gibb of deceit. He not only ignored the text but also claimed Dehlawi supported his ideas, while Dehlawi actually crushes those fantasies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما لا ريب فيه أن جب قد وقف على هذا النص الذي يأتي عقب تلك الجمل التي نقلها عنه ، وهو كما ترى ليس إلا تفصيلاً لها وبيـانـاً لمضمونها كما يعلم كل متأمل . فكيف أمكنه أن يتجاهله ويتغافل عنه ، بل لا يكتفي بذلك وحده حتى يزعم أنه إنما يستند في تقرير أفكاره إلى الدهلوي ، أي إلى هذا الرجل الذي يسحق هذه الأخيلة سحقاً كما ترى ؟ وبعد ، فهذه حقيقة المنهج العلمي المتبع لدى جمهرة الغربيين عندما يدخلون في مناقشة علمية مع الآخرين ، أو حينما يريدون أن يقيموا فرضية أو حقيقة ما ، أو عندما يحاولون استخراج علم أو إدراك يقين من نص أو وثيقة في التاريخ : طريقة استنتاجية أولاً ، ثم إخضاع البحث لمجرد الإرادة والرغبة ثانياً ، ثم القصد إلى تحريف النقول والنصوص ثالثاً
And there is no doubt that Gibb came across this text which comes after those sentences that he quoted from him, and it is, as you see, nothing but a detail of them and an explanation of their content, as every contemplative knows. So how could he ignore it and disregard it? Rather, he does not stop at that alone, but he claims that he only bases his ideas on Dehlawi, that is, on this man who utterly crushes these fantasies, as you see? Then, this is the reality of the scientific method followed by the majority of Westerners when they enter into a scientific discussion with others, or when they want to establish a hypothesis or a fact, or when they try to extract knowledge or acquire certainty from a text or historical document: a deductive method first, then subjecting research to mere will and desire second, then the intention to distort quotes and texts third.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a critique and a classification. The author first points out that the orientalist Gibb had access to the text from Dehlawi that follows the sentences he quoted, and that text explicitly clarifies and refutes Gibb's interpretation. Yet Gibb ignored it and even claimed to base his ideas on Dehlawi. But Dehlawi himself, according to the author, crushes those fantasies. Then the author generalizes to describe the supposed scientific method of Westerners. He enumerates three steps: first, they start with a deductive method (i.e., they assume a conclusion and then seek evidence); second, they subject their research to their own desires and wishes; third, they deliberately distort quotes and texts. The author presents this as a pattern, not an isolated incident.

Three Steps of Western Methodology
Buti enumerates three steps of Western scientific method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Buti's claim that the Western scientific method, when dealing with texts, follows three steps: deduction, desire-driven research, and distortion.

Core Idea
Western method is deductive, wishful, and distortive.

After critiquing Gibb's specific case, Buti broadens his critique to describe what he sees as a general pattern in Western scholarship. He enumerates three steps that characterize their approach to texts and historical evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Focus on the three points listed at the end.
The word 'ثم' (then) marks each step.
2
Identify the three steps
List the three steps: deductive method, subjecting research to desire, and distorting quotes.
Each step is a separate characteristic.
3
Understand each step
Consider what each step means: deduction means starting with a conclusion, desire means personal bias, distortion means intentional alteration.
4
Consider implications
Reflect on how this characterization challenges the common view of Western scholarship as objective.
Buti presents this as a pattern, not an isolated incident.

Criticism of Badawi's Misplaced Warnings
Buti criticizes Badawi for misdirecting methodological warnings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Buti's critique of Badawi's misplaced methodological warnings and his defense of Muslim scholars' scholarly integrity.

Core Idea
Badawi warns the wrong audience: Muslims, not Western distorters.

Buti acknowledges Badawi's correct methodological principles but exposes their misapplication. He contends that Badawi unjustly directs warnings to Muslim scholars who already uphold rigorous standards, while ignoring Western scholars who actually distort texts. This passage highlights the importance of fair critique and recognizing the strengths of Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Read Buti's initial praise
Notice that Buti begins by thanking Badawi for his correct warnings about contextual interpretation. This sets up the contrast.
Buti does not dismiss Badawi entirely; he agrees on the principle.
2
Identify the main criticism
Buti points out the irony: Badawi directs warnings to Muslim scholars, who already practice precision, rather than to Westerners who need them.
This is the core of Buti's complaint.
3
Examine the accusation against Muslims
Badawi claims that some Muslim scholars 'steal' Quranic verses to support modern ideas. Buti challenges this claim.
Buti demands a single example of such distortion.
4
Understand Buti's challenge
Buti asks Badawi to produce one example of a Muslim scholar distorting a text, implying none exists.
5
Reflect on the mislabeling of 'beliefs' and 'science'
Buti notes that Badawi dismisses Islamic truths as mere 'beliefs' while calling Western speculations about religion 'science'.
This shows Badawi's bias.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحينما نقف على هذه الحقائق ، وشيء من أمثلتها الكثيرة ، لا يسعنا إلا أن نشکر باحثاً مثل عبد الرحمن بدوي ، عندما يحذرنا ـ في صوفية سامية مستغرقة - في أعقاب حديثه عن المنهج الاستردادي لدى الغربيين من أن نفسر نصاً من النصوص التاريخية بغير لغة العصر التي كتبت بها ، وأن نتجاهل السياق والسباق ، أو نجازف في فهم إشارة أو عبارة على غير ما يرشد إليه سياق العبارة كلها . بيد أن العجيب في كلامه أنه إنما يتجه بهذه النصائح إلى علماء المسلمين الذين إليهم يعود فضل التنبيه إلى هذه الدقة والأمانة ، وإليهم يعود فضل تطبيقها على أتم وجه ، دون أن يتجه بشيء منها إلى هؤلاء الغربيين الذين أطنب في الإعجاب والحديث عن مناهجهم ممن عرضنا أمثلة مؤسفة لمنهجيتهم الآن ، لقد استعاض عن ذلك بالاتجاه إلى علماء المسلمين متخيلاً أن أحدهم يسرق الآية القرآنية أو الحديث النبوي - على حد قوله - لتأييد أقوال حديثة لا تمت في الواقع بأي صلة إليها إلا في ظاهر اللفظ . كنت آمل من عبد الرحمن بدوي وقد تجاهل ما يفعله هؤلاء بالمنهج عند البحث ، أن يذكر لنا مثلاً واحداً لباحث من علماء المسلمين ( ممن لا يقلدون أولياء أمره في اتباع منهجهم الاستردادي والتوسمي ( نقل نصاً فحرف فيه ، أو راح يستنبط الحقائق العلمية الخطيرة بحبال من الاستنتاج يشدها بمجرد الحدس والتخمين . وبعد ، فإن لك ، بعد استيعاب كل ما ذكرناه ، أن تعجب كل العجب لمن يسمى الحقائق الدينية التي وصل إليها الباحثون المسلمون بمنهجهم العلمي الذي أوضحناه : الاعتقادات أصحابه بالاعتقاديين . ثم يلتفت إلى ما يتصوره فلاسفة الغرب وملاحدتهم عن الدين فيسميه بالعلم ويسمي أرباب هذا ما
And when we stand upon these truths, and some of their many examples, we cannot but thank a researcher like Abd al-Rahman Badawi, when he warns us — in a sublime, absorbed Sufi state — in the wake of his talk about the recovery method (al-manhaj al-istirdadi) of the Westerners, against interpreting a historical text other than in the language of the era in which it was written, and against ignoring the context and the preceding context, or taking a risk in understanding an indication or expression otherwise than what the entire context of the expression indicates. But the wonder in his speech is that he directs these warnings only to Muslim scholars — who are the ones who have the merit of alerting to this precision and honesty, and the merit of applying it most perfectly — without directing any of it to those Westerners whom he has greatly admired and spoken about their methods, and whose unfortunate examples we have now presented of their methodology. He has substituted that by directing [warnings] to Muslim scholars, imagining that one of them steals the Quranic verse or the prophetic hadith — as he says — to support modern sayings that in reality bear no relation to them except in apparent wording. I had hoped from Abd al-Rahman Badawi — and he has ignored what these people do with methodology in research — that he would give us one example of a researcher from among the Muslim scholars (from those who do not imitate his masters in following their recovery and typological method) who transmitted a text and distorted it, or went on to deduce dangerous scientific truths with ropes of inference that he ties with mere guess and conjecture. Then, after comprehending all that we have mentioned, you may be greatly amazed at those who call the religious truths that the Muslim researchers arrived at by their scientific method which we have clarified: beliefs, and call its adherents 'theologians'. Then he turns to what the philosophers of the West and their atheists imagine about religion and calls it 'science', and calls its adherents '...'
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a criticism. The author first thanks Abd al-Rahman Badawi for his warnings about interpreting historical texts according to their era and context. But then he points out the irony: Badawi directs these warnings solely to Muslim scholars, who are already the ones who uphold these principles, while neglecting to warn Western scholars, whose methodology the author has just criticized. Furthermore, Badawi claims that some Muslim scholars 'steal' Quranic verses or hadiths to support modern ideas. The author challenges Badawi to produce a single example of a Muslim scholar distorting a text. Instead, Badawi misdirects his critique. Finally, the author expresses amazement at those who label the religious truths established by Muslim scholars through their sound method as mere 'beliefs' and call their adherents 'theologians' (in a pejorative sense), while considering Western philosophical speculations about religion as 'science'. The text ends mid-sentence, but the point is clear.

Misconception: Muslim scholars lack methodological rigor and need external warning.
Correction: Buti argues that Muslim scholars are the originators of textual precision and honesty, and they already apply it perfectly.

A) Badawi fails to thank Western scholars.

D) Badawi ignores the context of Quranic verses.

Key Takeaway
Buti defends Muslim scholars' methodological rigor and criticizes Badawi for unfairly accusing them of textual distortion and for mislabeling Islamic truths as mere beliefs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Badawi's Misguided Methodological Advice
Buti acknowledges Badawi's warning but critiques its misdirection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Buti acknowledges Badawi's valid methodological principles while critiquing their misdirection.

Core Idea
Badawi warns the wrong audience about methodological errors.

Buti begins by acknowledging that Badawi's warnings about historical text interpretation are sound. However, he immediately notes the irony: these warnings are directed at Muslim scholars who already practice them, while Western scholars who need them are ignored.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Buti starts with appreciation before delivering the critique.
The acknowledgment is sincere but quickly qualified.
2
Identify the two targets of Badawi's advice
Buti says Badawi directs his advice to Muslim scholars, not to Western scholars. Ask yourself: why would Buti find this strange?
Recall the context: Buti has just given examples of flawed Western methodologies.
3
Reflect on the double standard
Consider the implication: Badawi praises Western methodologies but criticizes Muslims, even though Muslims are the ones who uphold textual precision.
This sets up the deeper critique in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحينما نقف على هذه الحقائق، وشيء من أمثلتها الكثيرة، لا يسعنا إلا أن نشكر باحثاً مثل عبد الرحمن بدوي، عندما يحذرنا – في صوفية سامية مستغرقة – في أعقاب حديثه عن المنهج الاستردادي لدى الغربيين من أن نفسر نصاً من النصوص التاريخية بغير لغة العصر التي كتبت بها، وأن نتجاهل السياق والسباق، أو نجازف في فهم إشارة أو عبارة على غير ما يرشد إليه سياق العبارة كلها.

بيد أن العجيب في كلامه أنه إنما يتجه بهذه النصائح إلى علماء المسلمين الذين إليهم يعود فضل التنبيه إلى هذه الدقة والأمانة، وإليهم يعود فضل تطبيقها على أتم وجه، دون أن يتجه بشيء منها إلى هؤلاء الغربيين الذين أطنب في الإعجاب والحديث عن مناهجهم ممن عرضنا أمثلة مؤسفة لمنهجيتهم الآن. لقد استعاض عن ذلك بالاتجاه إلى علماء المسلمين متخيلاً أن أحدهم يسرق الآية القرآنية أو الحديث النبوي – على حد قوله – لتأييد أقوال حديثة لا تمت في الواقع بأي صلة إليها إلا في ظاهر اللفظ.

كنت آمل من عبد الرحمن بدوي وقد تجاهل ما يفعله هؤلاء بالمنهج عند البحث، أن يذكر لنا مثلاً واحداً لباحث من علماء المسلمين (ممن لا يقلدون أولياء أمره في اتباع منهجهم الاستردادي والتوسمي) نقل نصاً فحرف فيه، أو راح يستنبط الحقائق العلمية الخطيرة بحبال من الاستنتاج يشدها بمجرد الحدس والتخمين.

وبعد، فإن لك، بعد استيعاب كل ما ذكرناه، أن تعجب كل العجب لمن يسمي الحقائق الدينية التي وصل إليها الباحثون المسلمون بمنهجهم العلمي الذي أوضحناه: الاعتقادات، وأصحابه بالاعتقاديين. ثم يلتفت إلى ما يتصوره فلاسفة الغرب وملاحدتهم عن الدين فيسميه بالعلم ويسمي أرباب هذا التصور: العلميين! .. أي أن الدين، كما فهمه جب مثلاً بلا منهجيته التي رأيناها هو العلم، وتفكيره في ذلك علمي. وأما الدين كما فهمه علماء المسلمين طبقاً للمنهج العلمي الذي أوضحناه فهو مجرد اعتقاد، وتفكيرهم محض عمل اعتقادي!.

(1) انظر: مناهج البحث العلمي لعبد الرحمن بدوي: ص 307 و 308.
When we come to know these truths, and some of their many examples, we cannot but thank a researcher like Abd al-Rahman Badawi, when he warns us – in sublime immersed mysticism – in the wake of his talk about the restorative methodology of the Westerners, against interpreting a historical text in a language other than that of the era in which it was written, and against ignoring context and preceding context, or taking a risk in understanding an allusion or phrase in a way other than what the context of the entire statement indicates. However, the strange thing in his words is that he directs these pieces of advice to Muslim scholars, who are the ones to whom credit goes for drawing attention to this precision and trustworthiness, and to whom credit goes for applying them fully, without directing any of them to those Westerners of whose methodologies he spoke at length with admiration – we have now presented unfortunate examples of their methodology. He substituted that by turning to Muslim scholars, imagining that one of them steals the Quranic verse or the prophetic hadith – as he puts it – to support modern statements that have no real connection to them except in apparent wording. I had hoped from Abd al-Rahman Badawi, while he ignored what these people do with methodology in research, that he would mention for us a single example of a researcher from Muslim scholars (who do not imitate his masters in following their restorative and typological methodology) who transmitted a text and distorted it, or went on to deduce serious scientific facts with ropes of inference that he ties by mere guesswork and conjecture. After that, after comprehending all that we have mentioned, you may marvel greatly at him who calls the religious truths that Muslim researchers reached by their scientific methodology which we explained: 'beliefs', and their possessors 'believers'. Then he turns to what the philosophers of the West and their atheists imagine about religion and calls it 'science', and calls the possessors of this conception: 'scientists'! That is, religion as understood by Gibb, for example, with his methodology that we have seen, is science, and his thinking in that is scientific. But religion as understood by Muslim scholars according to the scientific methodology we explained is mere belief, and their thinking is purely a work of belief! (1) See: Research Methodologies by Abd al-Rahman Badawi, pp. 307-308.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a critique and refutation of a double standard. It begins by acknowledging that Abd al-Rahman Badawi gives useful warnings about interpreting historical texts: we must use the language of the era, consider context, and avoid guesswork. However, Buti finds it strange that Badawi directs these warnings only to Muslim scholars, who already practice such precision and trustworthiness, and neglects to mention the Western scholars whose flawed methodologies Buti has just given examples of. Buti then states that Badawi instead accuses Muslim scholars of 'stealing' Quranic verses or hadiths to support modern ideas, which have only a superficial connection. Buti expresses his wish that Badawi would provide a single example of a Muslim scholar (who does not blindly imitate his superiors) who distorted a text or used flimsy inferences. After that, Buti marvels at Badawi's labeling: he calls the religious truths attained by Muslim scholars with their sound methodology mere 'beliefs' and their practitioners 'believers', while he calls the Western philosophers' and atheists' conceptions of religion 'science' and their proponents 'scientists'. Buti gives the example of Gibb: Gibb's understanding of religion, with his flawed methodology, is called science, whereas the Muslim scholars' understanding, based on a true scientific methodology, is dismissed as mere belief. This is the core of the double standard.

Key Terms
المنهج الاستردادي
السياق والسباق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Buti fully agrees with Badawi's methodological advice.
Correction: Buti acknowledges the advice but immediately critiques its misdirection, showing that Badawi is inconsistent.

Checkpoint
What does Buti find 'strange' (عجيب) about Badawi's advice?
A) That Badawi gives useful warnings about interpreting historical texts.
B) That Badawi directs his advice to Muslim scholars rather than to Western scholars who need it.
C) That Badawi mentions the importance of context and language.
D) That Badawi praises Western methodologies.

Key Takeaway
Badawi's methodological warnings are sound but misdirected: they should target Western scholars, not the Muslims who already uphold textual precision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read Badawi's warning about interpreting texts out of context, did you feel a sting of guilt? In what area of your life—studying a hadith, reading a scholar's opinion, or even understanding a verse—have you been guilty of this? Will you change how you read the Quran or hadith from now on?
?
Buti criticizes Badawi for accusing Muslim scholars without evidence. Have you ever accused a fellow Muslim of distorting the faith without first checking your own assumptions? What will you do the next time you hear someone interpret a text in a way you disagree with?
?
Badawi's advice is good but he gave it to the wrong people. Where in your life do you give good advice to those who don't need it, while ignoring those who do? Commit to redirecting one piece of counsel this week.

The Double Standard: 'Beliefs' vs. 'Science'
Buti challenges Badawi and exposes the labeling double standard.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will see how Buti exposes Badawi's double standard in labeling Muslim scholarship as 'belief' and Western philosophy as 'science'.

Core Idea
Badawi calls Muslim truths 'belief' and Western guesses 'science'.

In this passage, Buti pushes further. He challenges Badawi to provide evidence of Muslim scholars distorting texts, then unveils the ultimate double standard: Badawi labels the sound findings of Muslim scholars as mere 'beliefs', while calling the speculative ideas of Western atheists 'science'.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice Buti's challenge for a single example of Muslim scholarly distortion, then the labeling critique.
The tone shifts from critique to outright condemnation.
2
Identify the labeling double standard
Buti contrasts what Badawi calls 'beliefs' (Muslim truths) with what he calls 'science' (Western speculations). Ask yourself: what is the basis for this distinction?
Buti argues that Muslim methodology is scientific, while Western approaches are often guesswork.
3
Reflect on the example of Gibb
Buti gives the example of Gibb: his understanding is called science, while the Muslim understanding is called belief. Consider the injustice of this.
This exemplifies the double standard.

وبعد، فإن لك، بعد استيعاب كل ما ذكرناه، أن تعجب كل العجب لمن يسمي الحقائق الدينية التي وصل إليها الباحثون المسلمون بمنهجهم العلمي الذي أوضحناه: الاعتقادات، وأصحابه بالاعتقاديين. ثم يلتفت إلى ما يتصوره فلاسفة الغرب وملاحدتهم عن الدين فيسميه بالعلم ويسمي أرباب هذا التصور: العلميين! .. أي أن الدين، كما فهمه جب مثلاً بلا منهجيته التي رأيناها هو العلم، وتفكيره في ذلك علمي. وأما الدين كما فهمه علماء المسلمين طبقاً للمنهج العلمي الذي أوضحناه فهو مجرد اعتقاد، وتفكيرهم محض عمل اعتقادي!.

(1) انظر: مناهج البحث العلمي لعبد الرحمن بدوي: ص 307 و 308.
When we come to know these truths, and some of their many examples, we cannot but thank a researcher like Abd al-Rahman Badawi, when he warns us – in sublime immersed mysticism – in the wake of his talk about the restorative methodology of the Westerners, against interpreting a historical text in a language other than that of the era in which it was written, and against ignoring context and preceding context, or taking a risk in understanding an allusion or phrase in a way other than what the context of the entire statement indicates. However, the strange thing in his words is that he directs these pieces of advice to Muslim scholars, who are the ones to whom credit goes for drawing attention to this precision and trustworthiness, and to whom credit goes for applying them fully, without directing any of them to those Westerners of whose methodologies he spoke at length with admiration – we have now presented unfortunate examples of their methodology. He substituted that by turning to Muslim scholars, imagining that one of them steals the Quranic verse or the prophetic hadith – as he puts it – to support modern statements that have no real connection to them except in apparent wording. The Western Division over Christianity and the Rejection of Reason
Buti explains the deep reason for failure of Western research methods through their division over Christianity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Buti explains the failure of Western research methods as stemming from their bifurcated stance toward Christianity and the consequent abandonment of sound reason by the religious group.

Core Idea
When false religion confronts reason, believers abandon reason not religion.

Buti invites us to investigate the deep reason behind the failure of Western research methodologies. He argues that Westerners are split over Christianity: the religious cannot reconcile its doctrines with reason, yet cannot abandon religion altogether. Faced with an irreconcilable choice, they sacrifice sound reason to keep their faith. This reveals why their intellectual methods are fundamentally flawed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with an open mind
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation without analysing. Let Buti's argument flow naturally. Notice the direct address to the 'rational reader'.
Buti is not attacking Christianity per se, but diagnosing a methodological problem.
2
Identify the two groups of Westerners
Buti divides Westerners into two groups: the religious (المتدينون) and the rejecters (الجاحدون). See how he defines each.
3
Focus on the dilemma of the religious group
The religious could not digest their religion through reason; their religion openly contradicts reason and science. Yet human nature presses them to hold onto religion for moral values.
4
Understand the binary choice and its outcome
They had only two options: reject the false religion or reject sound reason. They chose the latter, becoming 'believers' (اعتقادي) in the sense of clinging to belief at reason's expense.
This choice is the core of Buti's explanation for methodological failure.
5
Consider the incomplete part about non-believers
The passage breaks off mid-sentence when describing the non-believers' choice. Reflect on what you think Buti would say about their path.
The argument is incomplete, but the pattern is clear: both groups start from a flawed position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكن أنت أيها القارئ العاقل باحثاً موضوعياً نزيهاً، وأطلق على هذه الطائفة من الناس (عرباً كانوا أم أعاجم، ومسلمين كانوا أم غير مسلمين) الصفة التي يوحي بها النظر العلمي المجرد.

ذلك، فتعال أحدثك عن السبب العميق لهذه الظاهرة العجيبة في إخفاق مناهج البحث عند الغربيين: الغربيون، ينقسمون في موقفهم من الدين المسيحي إلى قسمين: متدينين قد استسلموا له وآمنوا بكل مضموناته وأحكامه، وجاحدين لا يستسلمون له ولا ينساقون إلى اتباعه والتدين به.

فأما المتدينون منهم، فلم يستطيعوا أن يهضموا عقائد دينهم ومقوماته كلها عن طريق العلم والعقل (إذ هو يجابه العقل والعلم في كثير من مقتضياتها مجابهة صريحة لا تحتمل التوفيق والتأويل) ولكنهم وجدوا أيضاً أن فطرتهم الإنسانية تلح عليهم في البحث عن دين يتمسكون به ومعبود يدينون له، وأيقنوا أن كثيراً من القيم الأخلاقية لا ضمانة لتحقيقها إلا ضمانة الدين وسلطانه في النفس. فأصبحوا بين أمرين لا ثالث لها أمامهم وفي بيئتهم: إما أن يرفضوا الدين الباطل أو أن يرفضوا العقل الصحيح. ولكنهم آثروا الثاني على الأول، فرفضوا العقل الصحيح ولم يرفضوا الدين الباطل. فكانوا بذلك حقاً (اعتقاديين).

وأما غير المتدينين منهم، فقد آثروا رفض الدين الباطل على رفض العقل
So be you, O rational reader, an objective impartial researcher, and apply to this group of people (whether Arabs or non-Arabs, Muslims or non-Muslims) the quality that pure scientific observation suggests. That is, come let me tell you about the deep reason for this strange phenomenon in the failure of research methodologies among the Westerners: The Westerners, in their stance towards the Christian religion, are divided into two groups: the religious who have surrendered to it and believed in all its contents and rulings, and the rejecters who do not surrender to it nor are led to follow it and become religious. As for the religious among them, they could not digest all the doctrines and fundamentals of their religion through science and reason (for it confronts reason and science in many of their requirements with a clear confrontation that does not allow for reconciliation or interpretation), but they also found that their human nature presses them to search for a religion to cling to and a deity to worship, and they became certain that many moral values have no guarantee for their realization except the guarantee of religion and its authority in the soul. So they came to be between two matters with no third for them before them and in their environment: either to reject the false religion or to reject sound reason. But they chose the second over the first, so they rejected sound reason and did not reject the false religion. Thus they were truly 'believers' (i'tiqadiyyun). As for the non-religious among them, they chose to reject the false religion over rejecting reason
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is an analysis of the deep cause behind the failure of Western research methodologies. Buti first addresses the reader directly, urging objective examination and to label people according to pure scientific observation. Then he explains the root cause: Westerners' stance toward Christianity splits them into two groups: believers (those who fully accept it) and rejecters (those who do not). The believers could not reconcile their religion's doctrines with reason and science because Christianity openly contradicts them. However, their human nature still demanded a religion and moral values that only religion can guarantee. So they were faced with a binary choice: reject their false religion or reject sound reason. They chose to reject sound reason, thus becoming 'believers' (i'tiqadiyyun) in a sense – they held onto belief but abandoned reason. The non-believers, on the other hand, chose to reject the false religion rather than reject reason. The passage ends here, leaving the conclusion about the non-believers incomplete. Buti's point is that both groups, due to their flawed initial starting point (a false religion or its rejection), develop flawed methodologies.

Key Terms
المتدينون
الجاحدون
العقل الصحيح
الدين الباطل
الاعتقادي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Buti is simply criticizing Christianity as a false religion.
Correction: Buti's aim is to diagnose a methodological failure: the religious group's abandonment of reason in order to retain a religion that opposes reason leads to flawed research methods. Christianity is an example, not the target.

Checkpoint
According to Buti, what did the religious Westerners (المتدينون) choose when faced with the contradiction between their religion and reason?
A) They rejected their false religion and kept sound reason.
B) They rejected sound reason and kept their false religion.
C) They tried to reconcile the two through interpretation.
D) They abandoned both religion and reason.

Key Takeaway
The failure of Western research methods stems from a prior failure: when religion contradicts reason, the religious choose to abandon reason rather than religion, corrupting their intellectual foundation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held onto a belief—whether inherited from family, culture, or a teacher—even when your own reason told you it might be false? What would it cost you to honestly examine that belief with sound reason, and are you willing to pay that price today?
?
Buti diagnoses the Western religious as choosing to abandon reason rather than their false religion. Which area of your life—faith, politics, personal habits—are you most resistant to applying objective reasoning, afraid of what you might lose? Will you allow that fear to dictate your intellectual integrity?
?
If you were to apply the same unbiased research method Buti calls for to your own religious understanding, would you find any points where you've accepted something without evidence or clung to tradition over truth? What would change if you committed to aligning your beliefs with reason and revelation, no matter the cost?

Scientific vs. Creedal: A False Dichotomy
Al-Bouti introduces the false dichotomy between 'scientific' and 'creedal' approaches to religion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Bouti distinguishes between the Western 'scientific' approach to religion and the Muslim scholarly approach, which he calls 'creedal' in a positive sense.

Core Idea
Labels like 'scientific' and 'creedal' are not neutral; they reflect underlying methodologies.

Al-Bouti opens by challenging the common dichotomy between science and religion as applied to Islam. He points out that Western scholars often label their own approach as 'scientific' while dismissing Muslim scholarly engagement with religion as mere 'creedal' work. But this is a reversal of reality: Muslim scholars use a rigorous method, while Westerners abandon reason when it conflicts with their faith. This lesson sets the stage for his deeper critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the two views
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the contrast al-Bouti draws between how religion is understood by Westerners (like Gibb) and by Muslim scholars.
The author uses the terms 'scientific' and 'creedal' in quotation marks to flag their contested meanings.
2
Identify the key distinction
What does al-Bouti say is the difference? According to him, Westerners claim their approach is scientific, but Muslim scholars' approach is 'mere belief'. He then invites the reader to apply objective observation.
3
Reflect on the reader's role
Al-Bouti addresses the reader as 'rational' and 'objective'. Why does he do this? He is setting up an expectation of impartial inquiry.
This is a call to avoid blind partisanship.

أوربا. فهؤلاء الناس رأوا أن الدين في أوربا له تفسيران: تفسير إيجابي هو في حقيقته تفسير اعتقادي محض، وتفسير سلبي هو، كما يسمونه هناك، تفسير علمي محض. فجاؤوا بهذين التفسيرين من هناك وأطلقوهما على الدين الإسلامي هنا، لا لشيء، ولكن لمجرد أن يتكامل الظل ويأتي التقليد محكماً من سائر أطرافه. فهذا هو السبب العميق لهذه الظاهرة العجيبة عند هؤلاء الناس. وليس يهمنا إطلاقاً أمر هذا الصنف من الناس، بعد أن يعلم العاقل المتأمل الحر، من كل ما أوضحته في هذا التمهيد، أن الإسلام لا يعني تلك المعتقدات التي خاصمت أوربا العقل في سبيلها، وأن الإسلام يقوم في مبادئه الاعتقادية كلها على منهج علمي دقيق نزيه لا تخطه إلا يد العقل وحده دون أن يكون ثمة سلطان لعصبية أو رغبة في اعتقاد أو تقليد واتباع.
The conception: the 'scientists'! That is, religion, as understood by Gibb for example without his methodology which we saw, is science, and his thinking in that is scientific. As for religion as understood by Muslim scholars according to the scientific method we have clarified, it is mere belief, and their thinking is purely credal work! So be you, O rational reader, an objective impartial researcher, and apply to this group of people — whether Arabs or non-Arabs, Muslims or non-Muslims — the attribute suggested by abstract scientific observation. That, so come let me tell you about the profound cause of this strange phenomenon among these people: The Westerners are divided in their attitude toward the Christian religion into two groups: religious people who have surrendered to it and believed in all its contents and rulings, and deniers who do not surrender to it nor are led to follow it and be religious by it. As for the religious among them, they could not digest all the doctrines and principles of their religion through science and reason (since it confronts reason and science in many of its requirements in a clear confrontation that does not allow reconciliation or interpretation), but they also found that their human nature urged them to seek a religion to cling to and a deity to worship, and they became certain that many moral values have no guarantee for their realization except the guarantee of religion and its authority in the soul. So they found themselves between two options, no third before them and in their environment: either to reject false religion or to reject correct reason. But they preferred the second over the first, so they rejected correct reason and did not reject false religion. Thus they were truly 'creedalists'. As for the non-religious among them, they preferred rejecting false religion over rejecting correct reason. But they were satisfied from the requirements of correct reason with refuting their religion and interpreting it according to their imagination and opinion, without paying attention to the true religion, before which reason and science bow to all its principles and rulings. They were prevented from that by another feeling — the feeling of European fanaticism and subjectivity and the constant caution that Muslims might once again return to the sovereignty of the world, as they once were. So these were given the name 'scientists'. Then you know that among Arabs and Muslims there are people whom you count as humans who speak, reason, and talk, but in reality they are nothing but pale extended shadows moving with the movements of Europe and the ideas of Europe and the philosophy of Europe. These people saw that religion in Europe has two interpretations: a positive interpretation which in reality is a purely credal interpretation, and a negative interpretation which, as they call it there, is a purely scientific interpretation. So they brought these two interpretations from there and applied them to the Islamic religion here, for no reason, but merely so that the shadow is complete and imitation comes firmly from all its sides. This is the profound cause of this strange phenomenon among these people. And we are not at all concerned with the matter of this type of people, after the free thoughtful person knows, from all that I have clarified in this introduction, that Islam does not mean those beliefs that Europe fought reason for the sake of, and that Islam in all its doctrinal principles is based on a precise, impartial scientific method that nothing traces except the hand of reason alone, without any authority of fanaticism or desire for belief or imitation and following.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of Western attitudes toward religion and their misguided application to Islam. The author, Ramadan al-Bouti, begins by challenging the common label 'scientists' (العلميين) as applied to certain people, contrasting it with how Muslim scholars understand religion—as a matter of belief (اعتقاد) based on a scientific method. He then divides Westerners into two groups: the religious (المتدينون) who, faced with contradictions between Christian doctrines and reason, chose to abandon reason rather than their religion, thus becoming 'creedalists' (اعتقاديين); and the non-religious (غير المتدينين) who rejected their religion but limited their use of reason to refuting it, ignoring the true religion (Islam) due to European bias and fear of Muslim resurgence, thus being called 'scientists' (العلميين). Al-Bouti then criticizes certain Arabs and Muslims who blindly imitate these Western categories, applying them to Islam without understanding that Islam's beliefs are fully compatible with reason. He concludes that Islam is fundamentally different: its doctrinal principles are based on a precise, impartial scientific method guided solely by reason, free from fanaticism or blind imitation. The argument unfolds in four stages: (1) definition of the problem; (2) analysis of Western religious and secular stances; (3) critique of Arab imitators; (4) affirmation of Islam's rational methodology.

Key Terms
اعتقادي
علمي
منهج علمي
عقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Bouti, how do Muslim scholars understand religion compared to Western figures like Gibb?
A) Muslim scholars also consider it science.
B) Muslim scholars consider it mere belief (creedal).
C) Muslim scholars reject all religion.
D) Muslim scholars follow a non-rational method.

Key Takeaway
The labels 'scientific' and 'creedal' are not objective; they depend on one's underlying methodology and assumptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter labels like 'scientific' or 'religious' in discussions about Islam, do you accept them at face value, or do you pause to ask what methodology they imply? Be honest: have you ever used a label to dismiss a viewpoint without examining its underlying assumptions?
?
Al-Bouti calls you to be 'an objective impartial researcher' — does your pursuit of truth truly hold that standard? Where in your life do you let loyalty to a group (Arab/non-Arab, Muslim/non-Muslim) override honest assessment of evidence?
?
Imagine reading this passage while sitting in a university lecture where a professor says 'Islamic thought is creedal, not scientific.' Would you have the courage to question that claim based on what al-Bouti teaches here? What holds you back?

Method of Learning
intermediate
6 min
The Two Western Groups: Creedalists and Scientists
Al-Bouti explains the motivations of Western religious and secular groups.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Bouti's analysis of why Westerners are divided into two groups—religious 'creedalists' and secular 'scientists'—and why both fail to attain truth.

Core Idea
Both Western groups sacrifice either reason or religion; neither follows the straight path.

Al-Bouti now delves into the root cause of the strange phenomenon he observed earlier. He divides Westerners into two camps: the religious (المتدينون) who cling to Christianity even when it contradicts reason, and the non-religious (غير المتدينين) who reject Christianity but remain biased against Islam. He explains the motivations of each, showing how both depart from true rationality and objectivity. This analysis is crucial for understanding why Western attitudes cannot be applied to Islam.

Guided Path
1
Read about the two Western groups
Read the section where al-Bouti describes the religious and the non-religious Westerners. Pay attention to the causes he gives for their choices.
The author uses the term 'اعتقاديين' (creedalists) for the religious group and 'علميين' (scientists) for the secular group.
2
Analyze the religious group's choice
Why did the religious Westerners reject correct reason? According to al-Bouti, they faced a contradiction between their faith and reason, and they chose faith.
He says they 'preferred the second over the first'—i.e., they rejected reason.
3
Analyze the secular group's choice
Why did the secular Westerners not accept the true religion (Islam)? Al-Bouti points to European bias and fear.
They used reason only to refute their own religion, not to seek truth.
4
Compare both groups
Both groups made a mistake: one rejected reason, the other rejected the true religion. Neither was truly objective.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Western 'scientists' are truly rational and objective, and that the religious are simply faithful.

Checkpoint
Why did the religious Westerners reject correct reason according to al-Bouti?
A) They found no contradiction between their faith and reason.
B) They wanted to preserve moral values and felt forced to choose between faith and reason, choosing faith.
C) They were forced by the church to reject reason.
D) They converted to Islam.

Key Takeaway
Neither Western group achieved true rationality: the religious sacrificed reason for faith, and the secular sacrificed objective truth-seeking for bias.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Bouti describes the Western religious as sacrificing correct reason to keep their false religion. Think of an area in your life—perhaps a belief or practice you inherited—where you have refused to apply reason because it would unsettle your comfort. What would it cost you to honestly examine it in light of truth? Write down one step you can take this week to submit your inherited assumptions to sincere investigation.
?
The non-religious scientists were so clouded by bias against a group that they missed the truth that was right in front of them. Where are you blinded by prejudice—against a person, a sect, a culture, or even against Islam itself? How can you actively dismantle that bias today? Commit to one concrete action that breaks the pattern of partisanship.
?
Al-Bouti says that human nature (fitrah) urgently demands a religion and a deity. Have you truly let your fitrah speak, or have you silenced it with excuses, busyness, or intellectual pride? If you were to stop running from the deep hunger in your soul, what would you have to confront? What would it look like to say, 'My Lord, I need You'—not just in words but in surrender?

Blind Imitators and the True Islamic Method
Al-Bouti criticizes imitators and upholds Islam's rational methodology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Bouti's critique of Arabs and Muslims who uncritically adopt Western categories, and his affirmation that Islam's creedal principles are founded on a purely rational, scientific method.

Core Idea
True Islam is rational through and through; the clash is between Western irrationality and Islamic reason.

In the final part of the passage, al-Bouti turns his attention to a third group: Arabs and Muslims who blindly imitate Western classifications. These people take the Western dichotomy of 'creedal' vs. 'scientific' and apply it to Islam without understanding its unique foundation. Al-Bouti then delivers his central thesis: Islam does not suffer from the tension between faith and reason that plagues Christianity. Instead, all Islamic doctrinal principles are based on a precise, impartial scientific method guided solely by reason, free from fanaticism, desire, or blind imitation. This is the true 'scientific' approach.

Guided Path
1
Read about the Arab imitators
Read the passage describing Arabs and Muslims who are mere 'pale shadows' of Europe. Notice the strong language al-Bouti uses.
He considers them to be blindly imitating Western categories.
2
Identify their mistake
What did they do wrong? They took the two interpretations of religion from Europe and applied them to Islam, without realizing Islam is different.
3
Understand al-Bouti's conclusion about Islam
Read the final lines where al-Bouti affirms that Islam's doctrinal principles are based on a precise scientific method, guided by reason alone.
He contrasts this with the 'creedal' approach of Western Christianity.
4
Reflect on the implication
How does this reframe the debate? Islam does not require a sacrifice of intellect; it invites rational inquiry.

The conception: the 'scientists'! That is, religion, as understood by Gibb for example without his methodology which we saw, is science, and his thinking in that is scientific. As for religion as understood by Muslim scholars according to the scientific method we have clarified, it is mere belief, and their thinking is purely credal work! So be you, O rational reader, an objective impartial researcher, and apply to this group of people — whether Arabs or non-Arabs, Muslims or non-Muslims — the attribute suggested by abstract scientific observation. That, so come let me tell you about the profound cause of this strange phenomenon among these people: The Westerners are divided in their attitude toward the Christian religion into two groups: religious people who have surrendered to it and believed in all its contents and rulings, and deniers who do not surrender to it nor are led to follow it and be religious by it. As for the religious among them, they could not digest all the doctrines and principles of their religion through science and reason (since it confronts reason and science in many of its requirements in a clear confrontation that does not allow reconciliation or interpretation), but they also found that their human nature urged them to seek a religion to cling to and a deity to worship, and they became certain that many moral values have no guarantee for their realization except the guarantee of religion and its authority in the soul. So they found themselves between two options, no third before them and in their environment: either to reject false religion or to reject correct reason. But they preferred the second over the first, so they rejected correct reason and did not reject false religion. Thus they were truly 'creedalists'. As for the non-religious among them, they preferred rejecting false religion over rejecting correct reason. But they were satisfied from the requirements of correct reason with refuting their religion and interpreting it according to their imagination and opinion, without paying attention to the true religion, before which reason and science bow to all its principles and rulings. They were prevented from that by another feeling — the feeling of European fanaticism and subjectivity and the constant caution that Muslims might once again return to the sovereignty of the world, as they once were. So these were given the name 'scientists'. Then you know that among Arabs and Muslims there are people whom you count as humans who speak, reason, and talk, but in reality they are nothing but pale extended shadows moving with the movements of Europe and the ideas of Europe and the philosophy of Europe. These people saw that religion in Europe has two interpretations: a positive interpretation which in reality is a purely credal interpretation, and a negative interpretation which, as they call it there, is a purely scientific interpretation. So they brought these two interpretations from there and applied them to the Islamic religion here, for no reason, but merely so that the shadow is complete and imitation comes firmly from all its sides. This is the profound cause of this strange phenomenon among these people. And we are not at all concerned with the matter of this type of people, after the free thoughtful person knows, from all that I have clarified in this introduction, that Islam does not mean those beliefs that Europe fought reason for the sake of, and that Islam in all its doctrinal principles is based on a precise, impartial scientific method that nothing traces except the hand of reason alone, without any authority of fanaticism or desire for belief or imitation and following.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of Western attitudes toward religion and their misguided application to Islam. The author, Ramadan al-Bouti, begins by challenging the common label 'scientists' (العلميين) as applied to certain people, contrasting it with how Muslim scholars understand religion—as a matter of belief (اعتقاد) based on a scientific method. He then divides Westerners into two groups: the religious (المتدينون) who, faced with contradictions between Christian doctrines and reason, chose to abandon reason rather than their religion, thus becoming 'creedalists' (اعتقاديين); and the non-religious (غير المتدينين) who rejected their religion but limited their use of reason to refuting it, ignoring the true religion (Islam) due to European bias and fear of Muslim resurgence, thus being called 'scientists' (العلميين). Al-Bouti then criticizes certain Arabs and Muslims who blindly imitate these Western categories, applying them to Islam without understanding that Islam's beliefs are fully compatible with reason. He concludes that Islam is fundamentally different: its doctrinal principles are based on a precise, impartial scientific method guided solely by reason, free from fanaticism or blind imitation. The argument unfolds in four stages: (1) definition of the problem; (2) analysis of Western religious and secular stances; (3) critique of Arab imitators; (4) affirmation of Islam's rational methodology.

Correction: Al-Bouti explicitly states that Islam does not mean those beliefs that Europe fought reason for, and that its principles are fully rational.

Checkpoint
According to al-Bouti, what is the foundation of Islam's doctrinal principles?

B) A precise, impartial scientific method guided by reason alone.

Key Takeaway
Islam's foundation is rational and scientific, not based on blind faith or imitation, distinguishing it from Western religious attitudes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a belief about Islam purely because it was presented as 'modern' or 'scientific', without verifying it from the Qur'an and Sunnah? Where in your life do you risk becoming a 'pale shadow' of external influences? Commit to examining one such belief this week with the 'hand of reason alone.'
?
Al-Bouti says Islam's principles are traced by reason without fanaticism or desire. Is your faith truly built on rational conviction, or do you follow Islam out of habit, family tradition, or social comfort? What would change if you subjected each of your core beliefs to this test? Choose one belief to question honestly before your next prayer.
?
The passage dismisses blind imitators as 'not worth concerning ourselves with.' Do you have the same detachment when you see Muslims misrepresenting Islam according to foreign ideologies? Or do you get entangled in emotional debates? How would adopting al-Bouti's confident, reasoned stance affect your da'wah? Plan one concrete step to embody that confidence this month.

Addressing the Skeptic: Why Religion? — Buti's Methodological Approach
Buti presents a skeptical question about religion's necessity and outlines his methodological response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Buti addresses the skeptical question about the necessity of religion by reframing it as a question of God's existence, and why this methodological step is crucial before discussing creed.

Core Idea
Before asking why God requires worship, first ask if God exists.

Buti begins by presenting a common skeptical question: why does God burden us with religion? The question seems urgent, but Buti argues it is premature. In this passage, he explains why the proper response is not to answer directly but to redirect the discussion to the foundational issue of God's existence. This methodological move is essential for any meaningful theological inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Read the skeptical question
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the series of questions the skeptic raises. Notice the tone of complaint.
The question implies that God would be needy or unjust if He imposed religion.
2
Identify Buti's initial response
Buti says a sufficient answer is necessary before delving into creed. Then observe how he changes direction in the footnote.
The footnote explains the methodological principle.
3
Reflect on the method
Consider why Buti insists on referring the question back to the existence of God. How does this protect the discussion from futility?
This is a key lesson in apologetics: address foundational issues first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانيا: ما الذي أهون اللانتان را النقية الصحيحة عن الكون والحياة والتزام مقتضياتها؟

يظل بعض الناس، ممن لم تتهيأ لديهم ثقافة إسلامية كافية، يتساءل: ما وجه الحاجة أو الضرورة إلى أن يتعبدنا الله (على فرض وجوده) بهذا الدين ويلزمنا بكل هذا الذي يتضمنه من اعتقاد وعبادة وأحكام؟ وهلا يترك هذا الإله عباده أحراراً يقيمون حياتهم على الوجه الذي يريدون، وينظمونها حسب الشكل أو الطريقة التي يحبون؟.. وقد تمتد ببعضهم سلسلة هذا التساؤل فيسأل في ضيق وتعجب: وما حاجة الله في أن أحبس نفسي على عبادته العمر كله، وما الذي ينقصه أو يضره لو لم أفعل ذلك(۱)

ولابد من إجابة كافية شافية على هذا السؤال أولاً، وقبل الخوض في أي بحث من بحوث العقيدة الإسلامية. فلن تتهيأ الأذهان والعقول لاستقبال حقائق التوحيد ومقومات هذا الدين العقيدية ما لم تصف الرؤية أمامها، وتخلص الطريق إليها من كل الشوائب والعقبات والمعوقات.

فنقول في الجواب على ذلك: إن الله عز وجل حينما تعلقت إرادته بإيجاد هذا الكون بما فيه من الموجودات

(۱) نحن نؤثر من الناحية المنهجية عدم الإجابة على مثل هذه الأسئلة المتفرعة عن إنكار وجود الذات الإلهية، إذ إن من المستحيل إقناع السائل بأي جواب مادام أصل السؤال قائماً في ذهنه، وإنما يجب إهمال هذا السؤال والرجوع به إلى أصل الموضوع وهو البحث في وجود الله عز وجل. ذلك فقد أحببت أن أعرض للجواب على هذا السؤال في مقدمة بحوثنا المقبلة، تنويراً لذهن الباحث المؤمن لا إقناعاً لفكر المجادل الملحد.
Second: What which [unclear] the pure correct about the universe and life and the commitment to its requirements? Some people, lacking sufficient Islamic culture, continue to ask: What is the aspect of need or necessity that God (assuming His existence) should burden us with this religion and oblige us with all that it contains of belief, worship, and rulings? And why does this God not leave His servants free to establish their lives in the way they want, and organize them according to the form or method they like?.. And this chain of questioning may extend for some of them, so they ask in distress and astonishment: And what need does God have that I should confine myself to worshipping Him for my entire life, and what does He lack or what harm would come to Him if I did not do that? (1) There must be a sufficient and satisfying answer to this question first, before delving into any research of Islamic creed. For minds and intellects will not be prepared to receive the truths of tawhid and the components of this creedal religion unless the vision is clear before them, and the path to them is cleared of all impurities, obstacles, and impediments. So we say in answer to that: Indeed, Allah Almighty, when His will was attached to the creation of this universe including all existing things... (1) Methodologically, we prefer not to answer such questions derived from the denial of the existence of the Divine Essence, for it is impossible to convince the questioner with any answer as long as the root of the question remains in his mind. Rather, this question must be set aside and referred back to the original topic, which is the investigation of the existence of Allah Almighty. Therefore, I wanted to present the answer to this question in the introduction of our upcoming researches, as an enlightenment for the mind of the believing researcher, not as a persuasion for the thought of the atheist disputant.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage consists of two parts: first, a common skeptical question about the necessity of religion; second, the author's methodological response. The author begins by expressing the question as posed by those lacking sufficient Islamic culture: why would God (assuming His existence) burden humans with religion, rather than leaving them free? The question extends to a complaint about the obligation of lifelong worship, implying God does not benefit from it. The author then states that a sufficient answer is necessary before delving into creedal discussions. However, in his answer, he takes a methodological detour: he argues that such questions presuppose the denial of God's existence, so the proper approach is to ignore the derivative question and refer back to the root issue—the existence of God. He concludes that his forthcoming answer is intended as enlightenment for the believer, not persuasion for the atheist disputant. This reveals the author's priority: establishing divine existence before addressing the 'why' of religious obligations.

Correction: Buti does not dismiss it; he reframes it as a derivative question that can only be answered after establishing God's existence.

Key Takeaway
Before answering why God requires worship, one must first establish that God exists—otherwise the discussion lacks foundation.

?

What would change if you first settled the question of His existence?

The Need for Religion: Vicegerency and Human Faculties
Buti answers why God requires worship by explaining man's role as vicegerent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Buti's argument that religion is necessary not for God's benefit but to guide man's dual-natured faculties in fulfilling his role as vicegerent on earth.

Core Idea
Religion is a human necessity, not a divine need, to harness our faculties for good.

This lesson tackles a common question about the purpose of religious obligations: why does God impose them? Buti's answer delves into God's wisdom in creation, particularly man's appointment as vicegerent (khalīfah) and the faculties granted to him. The passage shows that these faculties are double-edged, requiring divine guidance to produce order and good, thus establishing the necessity of religion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice the questions from those who see no need for religion: Why impose belief, worship, and rulings? Why not leave man free?
This objection stems from a lack of Islamic culture and a misunderstanding of God's wisdom.
2
Understand Buti's methodological caution
He notes that if the questioner denies God's existence, the proper approach is to first prove God's existence, not answer directly. But for the believing researcher, he proceeds.
This highlights the priority of foundational beliefs in theological discussion.
3
Grasp the concept of vicegerency
Buti explains that God created man as the master of the universe and entrusted him with its cultivation (istiʿmār). This is supported by Quranic verses.
Vicegerency is the key to understanding why man needs guidance.
4
Examine the faculties given to man
Identify the five faculties: intellect, selfishness, strength/management, and emotions. These equip man for his role but also carry great risk.
Each faculty has a positive and negative potential.
5
Reflect on the double-edged nature
Buti likens these faculties to a double-edged sword: proper use brings order and good; improper use brings chaos and misery. This sets the stage for the need for divine guidance.
This is the core of the argument for the necessity of religion.
